Hannibal</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right: 1em">Native&nbsp;name</th><td class="infobox-data"><div class="nickname" lang="phn"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1226385857">.mw-parser-output .script-phoenician{font-family:"Noto Sans Phoenician",sans-serif}</style><span dir="rtl" class="script-phoenician">𐤇𐤍𐤁𐤏𐤋</span>‎</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right: 1em">Born</th><td class="infobox-data">247 BC<br><div style="display:inline" <hr>
(see <a href="#Battle_record">Battle record</a> for a full list of battles commanded)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right: 1em">Spouse</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Imilce" title="Imilce">Imilce</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right: 1em">Children</th><td class="infobox-data">Possibly a son (Haspar)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right: 1em">Relations</th><td class="infobox-data"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409"><div class="plainlist"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hamilcar_Barca" title="Hamilcar Barca">Hamilcar Barca</a> (father)</li><li><a href="/wiki/Hasdrubal_(Barcid)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hasdrubal (Barcid)">Hasdrubal</a> (brother)</li><li><a href="/wiki/Mago_(Barcid)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mago (Barcid)">Mago</a> (brother)</li><li><a href="/wiki/Hasdrubal_the_Fair" title="Hasdrubal the Fair">Hasdrubal the Fair</a> (brother-in-law)</li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-right: 1em">Other&nbsp;work</th><td class="infobox-data">Politician</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Hannibal</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/əl/: 'le' in 'bottle'">əl</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Punic_language" title="Punic language">Punic</a>: <span lang="xpu" dir="rtl">𐤇𐤍𐤁𐤏𐤋</span>; 247 – between 183 and 181 BC) was a <a href="/wiki/Punic_people" title="Punic people">Punic</a>-<a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthaginian</a> general and statesman who commanded the forces of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Carthage" title="Ancient Carthage">Carthage</a> in their battle against the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Punic_War" title="Second Punic War">Second Punic War</a>. Hannibal lived during a period of great tension in the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean Basin</a>, triggered by the emergence of the Roman Republic as a great power with its defeat of Carthage in the <a href="/wiki/First_Punic_War" title="First Punic War">First Punic War</a>. <a href="/wiki/Revanchism" title="Revanchism">Revanchism</a> prevailed in Carthage, symbolized by the pledge that Hannibal made to his father to "never be a friend of <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>".
</p><p>In 218 BC Hannibal attacked Saguntum (modern <a href="/wiki/Sagunto" title="Sagunto">Sagunto</a>, Spain), an ally of Rome in <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a>, and this attack sparked the Second Punic War. Hannibal invaded Italy by <a href="/wiki/Hannibal%27s_crossing_of_the_Alps" title="Hannibal's crossing of the Alps">crossing the Alps</a> with North African <a href="/wiki/War_elephant" title="War elephant">war elephants</a>. In his first few years in Italy, as the leader of a Carthaginian and partially <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a> army, he won a succession of victories at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ticinus" title="Battle of Ticinus">Battle of Ticinus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Trebia" title="Battle of the Trebia">Trebia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lake_Trasimene" title="Battle of Lake Trasimene">Lake Trasimene</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cannae" title="Battle of Cannae">Cannae</a>, inflicting heavy losses on the Romans. Hannibal was distinguished for his ability to determine both his and his opponent's respective strengths and weaknesses, and to plan battles accordingly. His well-planned strategies allowed him to conquer and ally with several Italian cities that were previously allied to Rome. Hannibal occupied most of southern Italy for 15 years. The Romans, led by <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Fabius_Maximus_Verrucosus" title="Quintus Fabius Maximus Verrucosus">Fabius Maximus</a>, avoided directly engaging him, instead waging a war of <a href="/wiki/Attrition_warfare" title="Attrition warfare">attrition</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Fabian_strategy" title="Fabian strategy">Fabian strategy</a>). Carthaginian defeats in Hispania prevented Hannibal from being reinforced, and he was unable to win a decisive victory. A counter-invasion of North Africa, led by the Roman general <a href="/wiki/Scipio_Africanus" title="Scipio Africanus">Scipio Africanus</a>, forced him to return to Carthage. Hannibal was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Zama" title="Battle of Zama">Battle of Zama</a>, ending the war in a Roman victory.
</p><p>After the war, Hannibal successfully ran for the office of <a href="/wiki/Shophet" title="Shophet">sufet</a>. He enacted political and financial reforms to enable the payment of the war indemnity imposed by Rome. Those reforms were unpopular with members of the Carthaginian aristocracy and in Rome, and he fled into voluntary exile. During this time, he lived at the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid</a> court, where he acted as military advisor to <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_III_the_Great" title="Antiochus III the Great">Antiochus III the Great</a> in his war against Rome. Antiochus met defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Magnesia" title="Battle of Magnesia">Battle of Magnesia</a> and was forced to accept Rome's terms, and Hannibal fled again, making a stop in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Armenia_(antiquity)" title="Kingdom of Armenia (antiquity)">Kingdom of Armenia</a>. His flight ended in the court of <a href="/wiki/Bithynia" title="Bithynia">Bithynia</a>. He was betrayed to the Romans and, poisoning himself, died by suicide.
</p><p>Hannibal is considered one of the greatest military tacticians and generals of Western antiquity, alongside <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a>, Scipio Africanus, and <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhus_of_Epirus" title="Pyrrhus of Epirus">Pyrrhus</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>, Scipio asked Hannibal "who the greatest general was", to which Hannibal replied "either Alexander or Pyrrhus, then myself".<sup id="cite_ref-:1_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-1"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc">
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Name">Name</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hannibal_as_a_child,_accompanied_by_a_Roman_soldier.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Hannibal_as_a_child%2C_accompanied_by_a_Roman_soldier.jpg/250px-Hannibal_as_a_child%2C_accompanied_by_a_Roman_soldier.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="373" style="--mw-file-upright: 0.75" class="mw-file-element mw-file-upright" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Hannibal_as_a_child%2C_accompanied_by_a_Roman_soldier.jpg/500px-Hannibal_as_a_child%2C_accompanied_by_a_Roman_soldier.jpg 2x" data-file-width="671" data-file-height="1000"></a><figcaption>Circa 1850 engraving of <i>Young Hannibal</i> (left) by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Turner_(engraver)" title="Charles Turner (engraver)">Charles Turner</a></figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hannibal_(given_name)" title="Hannibal (given name)">Hannibal</a> was a common <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a> Phoenician-Carthaginian personal name. It is recorded in Carthaginian sources as <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r920966791">.mw-parser-output span.smallcaps{font-variant:small-caps}.mw-parser-output span.smallcaps-smaller{font-size:85%}</style><span class="smallcaps">ḥnbʿl</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuss1985[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidNvEK7kc3qnQCpgPA565_565]_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuss1985[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidNvEK7kc3qnQCpgPA565_565]-2"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Punic_language" title="Punic language">Punic</a>: <span lang="xpu" dir="rtl">𐤇𐤍𐤁𐤏𐤋</span>). It is a combination of the common Phoenician masculine given name <a href="/wiki/Hanno_(given_name)" title="Hanno (given name)">Hanno</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Semitic_languages" title="Northwest Semitic languages">Northwest Semitic</a> <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaanite</a> deity <a href="/wiki/Baal_Hammon" title="Baal Hammon">Baal Hammon</a><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> (lit, "lord") a major god of the Carthaginian's ancestral homeland of <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a> in Western Asia. Its precise vocalization remains a matter of debate. Suggested readings include <i>Ḥannobaʿal</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-brown_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown-4"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> <i>Ḥannibaʿl</i>, or <i>Ḥannibaʿal</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-benz_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-benz-5"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-baier_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baier-6"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> meaning "Baʿal/The lord is gracious", "Baʿal Has Been Gracious",<sup id="cite_ref-baier_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baier-6"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> or "The Grace of Baʿal".<sup id="cite_ref-benz_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-benz-5"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>  
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hellenic_historiography" title="Hellenic historiography">Greek historians</a> rendered the name as <i>Anníbas</i> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Ἀννίβας</span></span>).
The Phoenicians and Carthaginians, like many West Asian Semitic peoples, did not use hereditary surnames but were typically distinguished from others bearing the same name using <a href="/wiki/Patronymic" title="Patronymic">patronymics</a> or <a href="/wiki/Epithet" title="Epithet">epithets</a>. Although he is by far the most famous Hannibal, when further clarification is necessary he is usually referred to as "Hannibal, son of Hamilcar", or "Hannibal the Barcid", to his father, <a href="/wiki/Hamilcar_Barca" title="Hamilcar Barca">Hamilcar Barca</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/B-R-Q" class="mw-redirect" title="B-R-Q">Barca</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Punic_language" title="Punic language">Punic</a>: <span lang="xpu" dir="rtl">𐤁𐤓𐤒</span>, <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791"><span class="smallcaps">brq</span>) is a Semitic <a href="/wiki/Cognomen" title="Cognomen">cognomen</a> meaning "lightning" or "thunderbolt",<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> a surname acquired by Hamilcar on account of the swiftness and ferocity of his attacks. Barca is cognate with similar names for lightning found among the <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Israelites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Babylonians" class="mw-redirect" title="Babylonians">Babylonians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">Arameans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arab_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab people">Arabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amorites" title="Amorites">Amorites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moabites" class="mw-redirect" title="Moabites">Moabites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edomites" class="mw-redirect" title="Edomites">Edomites</a> and other fellow Asiatic Semitic peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>Although they did not inherit the surname from their father, Hamilcar's progeny are collectively known as the <a href="/wiki/Barcids" title="Barcids">Barcids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Modern historians occasionally refer to Hannibal's brothers as <a href="/wiki/Hasdrubal_Barca" title="Hasdrubal Barca">Hasdrubal Barca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mago_Barca" title="Mago Barca">Mago Barca</a> to distinguish them from the multitudes of other Carthaginians named Hasdrubal and Mago, but this practice is ahistorical and is rarely applied to Hannibal. 
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Background_and_early_career">Background and early career</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background and early career"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Carthage,_quarter_shekel,_237-209_BC,_SNG_BM_Spain_102.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Carthage%2C_quarter_shekel%2C_237-209_BC%2C_SNG_BM_Spain_102.jpg/250px-Carthage%2C_quarter_shekel%2C_237-209_BC%2C_SNG_BM_Spain_102.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="119" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Carthage%2C_quarter_shekel%2C_237-209_BC%2C_SNG_BM_Spain_102.jpg/500px-Carthage%2C_quarter_shekel%2C_237-209_BC%2C_SNG_BM_Spain_102.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="380"></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Carthaginian_currency" class="mw-redirect" title="Carthaginian currency">quarter shekel</a> of Carthage, perhaps minted in Spain. The obverse may depict Hannibal with the traits of a young <a href="/wiki/Melqart" title="Melqart">Melqart</a> (often associated with <a href="/wiki/Herakles" class="mw-redirect" title="Herakles">Herakles</a>), although this is improbable. It is more likely that it simply depicts the god himself. The reverse features one of his famous <a href="/wiki/War_elephants" class="mw-redirect" title="War elephants">war elephants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>Hannibal was one of the sons of Carthaginian general and statesman Hamilcar Barca and an unknown mother. He was most likely born in <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a>, one of many <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> regions colonised by the Canaanites from their homeland in Phoenicia. He had several sisters whose names are unknown and two brothers, Hasdrubal and Mago. His brothers-in-law were <a href="/wiki/Hasdrubal_the_Fair" title="Hasdrubal the Fair">Hasdrubal the Fair</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Numidians" title="Numidians">Numidian</a> king <a href="/wiki/Naravas" title="Naravas">Naravas</a>. He was still a child when his sisters married, and his brothers-in-law were close associates during his father's struggles in the <a href="/wiki/Mercenary_War" title="Mercenary War">Mercenary War</a> and the Punic conquest of the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>After Carthage's defeat in the <a href="/wiki/First_Punic_War" title="First Punic War">First Punic War</a>, Hamilcar set out to improve his family's and Carthage's fortunes. With that in mind and supported by <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A1diz" title="Cádiz">Gades</a>, Hamilcar began the subjugation of the tribes of the Iberian Peninsula. Carthage was in such a poor state at the time that it lacked a navy able to transport his army; instead, Hamilcar had to march his forces across Numidia towards the <a href="/wiki/Pillars_of_Hercules" title="Pillars of Hercules">Pillars of Hercules</a> and then cross the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">Polybius</a>, Hannibal much later said that when he came upon his father and begged to go with him, Hamilcar agreed and demanded that Hannibal swear that he would never be a friend of Rome as long as he lived. There is even an account of him at a very young age (9 years old) begging his father to take him to an overseas war. In the story, Hamilcar brought him to a sacrificial chamber. Hamilcar held Hannibal over the fire roaring in the chamber and made him swear that he would never be a friend of Rome. Other sources report that Hannibal told his father, "I swear so soon as age will permit...I will use fire and steel to arrest the destiny of Rome."<sup id="cite_ref-dodge_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dodge-14"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> According to the tradition, Hannibal's oath took place in <a href="/wiki/Peniscola" title="Peniscola">Peñíscola</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Hamilcar went about with the conquest of <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a>. When Hamilcar drowned<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> in battle, Hasdrubal succeeded to his command of the army with Hannibal (then 18 years old) serving as an officer under him. Hasdrubal pursued a policy of consolidation of Carthage's Iberian interests, even signing a treaty with Rome whereby Carthage would not expand north of the <a href="/wiki/Ebro" title="Ebro">Ebro</a> so long as Rome did not expand south of it.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Hasdrubal also endeavoured to consolidate Carthaginian power through diplomatic relationships with the <a href="/wiki/Iberians" title="Iberians">Iberians</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berbers</a> of the North African coasts.<sup id="cite_ref-brainfly1_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brainfly1-19"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Upon the assassination of Hasdrubal in 221&nbsp;BC, Hannibal at 26 years old was proclaimed commander-in-chief by the army and confirmed in his appointment by the Carthaginian government. Roman scholar <a href="/wiki/Livy" title="Livy">Livy</a> gives a depiction of the young Carthaginian: "No sooner had he arrived...the old soldiers fancied they saw Hamilcar in his youth given back to them; the same bright look; the same fire in his eye, the same trick of countenance and features. Never was one and the same spirit more skilful to meet opposition, to obey, or to command[.]"<sup id="cite_ref-brainfly1_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brainfly1-19"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:1868,_Mugeres_c%C3%A9lebres_de_Espa%C3%B1a_y_Portugal,_Himilce,_AB195_0021_(cropped).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/1868%2C_Mugeres_c%C3%A9lebres_de_Espa%C3%B1a_y_Portugal%2C_Himilce%2C_AB195_0021_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-1868%2C_Mugeres_c%C3%A9lebres_de_Espa%C3%B1a_y_Portugal%2C_Himilce%2C_AB195_0021_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="344" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/1868%2C_Mugeres_c%C3%A9lebres_de_Espa%C3%B1a_y_Portugal%2C_Himilce%2C_AB195_0021_%28cropped%29.jpg/500px-1868%2C_Mugeres_c%C3%A9lebres_de_Espa%C3%B1a_y_Portugal%2C_Himilce%2C_AB195_0021_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2569" data-file-height="3537"></a><figcaption>An 1868 illustration of <a href="/wiki/Imilce" title="Imilce">Imilce</a> and her son Haspar by Juan de Dios de la Rada</figcaption></figure>
<p>Livy records that Hannibal married a woman from <a href="/wiki/Castulo" title="Castulo">Castulo</a>, a powerful Spanish city closely allied with Carthage.<sup id="cite_ref-brainfly1_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brainfly1-19"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Roman poet <a href="/wiki/Silius_Italicus" title="Silius Italicus">Silius Italicus</a> names her as <a href="/wiki/Imilce" title="Imilce">Imilce</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Silius suggests a Greek origin for Imilce, but historian <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Charles-Picard" title="Gilbert Charles-Picard">Gilbert Charles-Picard</a> argues for a Punic heritage based on an etymology from the Semitic root m-l-k ('chief, the 'king').<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Silius also suggests the existence of a son,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> who is otherwise not attested by Livy, Polybius, or <a href="/wiki/Appian" title="Appian">Appian</a>. The son may have been named Haspar or Aspar,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> although this is disputed.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>After he assumed command, Hannibal spent two years consolidating his holdings and completing the conquest of Hispania, south of the Ebro.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In his first campaign, Hannibal stormed the <a href="/wiki/Olcades" title="Olcades">Olcades</a>' strongest centre, Alithia, which promptly led to their surrender, and brought Punic power close to the River <a href="/wiki/Tagus" title="Tagus">Tagus</a>. His following campaign in 220 BC was against the <a href="/wiki/Vaccaei" title="Vaccaei">Vaccaei</a> to the west, where he stormed the Vaccaen strongholds of Helmantice and Arbucala. On his return home, laden with many spoils, a coalition of Spanish tribes led by the <a href="/wiki/Carpetani" title="Carpetani">Carpetani</a> attacked, and Hannibal won his first major battlefield success and showed off his tactical skills at the battle of the River Tagus.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>Fearing the growing strength of Hannibal in Iberia, Rome made an alliance with <a href="/wiki/Sagunto" title="Sagunto">Saguntum</a>, which lay a considerable distance south of the River Ebro, and claimed the city as its <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">protectorate</a>. Hannibal perceived this as a breach of the treaty signed with Hasdrubal, and since he was already planning an attack on Rome, this was his way to start the war. So he <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Saguntum" title="Siege of Saguntum">laid siege to the city</a>, which fell after eight months.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Hannibal sent the booty from Saguntum to Carthage, a shrewd move which gained him much support from the government; Livy records that only <a href="/wiki/Hanno_II_the_Great" title="Hanno II the Great">Hanno II the Great</a> spoke against him.<sup id="cite_ref-brainfly1_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brainfly1-19"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In Rome, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Senate</a> reacted to this apparent violation of the treaty by dispatching a delegation to Carthage to demand whether Hannibal had destroyed Saguntum in accordance with orders from Carthage. The Carthaginian Senate responded with legal arguments observing the lack of ratification by either government for the treaty alleged to have been violated.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The delegation's leader, <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Fabius_Maximus_Verrucosus" title="Quintus Fabius Maximus Verrucosus">Quintus Fabius Maximus Verrucosus</a>, demanded Carthage choose between war and peace, to which his audience replied that Rome could choose. Fabius chose war.<sup id="cite_ref-brainfly1_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brainfly1-19"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Second_Punic_War_in_Italy_(218–204_BC)"><span id="Second_Punic_War_in_Italy_.28218.E2.80.93204_BC.29"></span>Second Punic War in Italy (218–204 BC)</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Second Punic War in Italy (218–204 BC)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_Punic_War" title="Second Punic War">Second Punic War</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Overland_journey_to_Italy">Overland journey to Italy</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Overland journey to Italy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hannibal%27s_crossing_of_the_Alps" title="Hannibal's crossing of the Alps">Hannibal's crossing of the Alps</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hannibal%27s_Travels_during_the_Second_Punic_War.png" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="a map of the western Mediterranean showing the route followed by the Carthaginians from Iberia to Italy" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Hannibal%27s_Travels_during_the_Second_Punic_War.png/250px-Hannibal%27s_Travels_during_the_Second_Punic_War.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="172" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Hannibal%27s_Travels_during_the_Second_Punic_War.png/500px-Hannibal%27s_Travels_during_the_Second_Punic_War.png 2x" data-file-width="3796" data-file-height="2605"></a><figcaption><div class="center">Hannibal's route from Iberia to Italy</div></figcaption></figure>
<p>A campaign to Italy was originally planned by Hasdrubal, who was stationed in the Iberian Peninsula for eight years until 221 BC. Soon, the Romans became aware of an alliance between Carthage and the <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celts</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Po_Valley" title="Po Valley">Po Valley</a> in northern Italy. When Hannibal arrived in the Po Valley, roughly 10,000 Celtic tribesmen joined his army.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The Celts were amassing forces to invade farther south in Italy, presumably with Carthaginian backing. Therefore, the Romans pre-emptively invaded the Po region in 225&nbsp;BC. By 220&nbsp;BC the Romans had annexed the area as <a href="/wiki/Cisalpine_Gaul" title="Cisalpine Gaul">Cisalpine Gaul</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Hasdrubal was assassinated around the same time (221&nbsp;BC), bringing Hannibal to the fore. It seems that the Romans lulled themselves into a false sense of security, having dealt with the threat of a Gallo-Carthaginian invasion, and perhaps knowing that the original Carthaginian commander had been killed. <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2026)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Hannibal departed Cartagena, Spain (New Carthage) in late spring of 218&nbsp;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> He fought his way through the northern tribes to the foothills of the <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a>, subduing the tribes through clever mountain tactics and stubborn fighting. He left a detachment of 20,000 troops to garrison the conquered region. At the Pyrenees, he released 11,000 Iberian troops who showed reluctance to leave their homeland. Hannibal reportedly entered <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> with 40,000 foot soldiers and 12,000 horsemen.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Hannibal recognized that he still needed to cross the Pyrenees, the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>, and many large rivers.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Additionally, he would have to contend with opposition from the Gauls, whose territory he passed through. Starting in the spring of 218 BC, he crossed the Pyrenees, and by conciliating the Gaulish chiefs along his passage before the Romans could take any measures to bar his advance, he was able to reach the <a href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne" title="Rhône">Rhône</a> by September. Hannibal's army numbered 38,000 infantry, 8,000 cavalry, and 38 elephants, almost none of which would survive the harsh conditions of the Alps.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Heinrich_Leutemann_-_Hannibals_%C3%9Cbergang_%C3%BCber_die_Alpen_(cropped).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Heinrich_Leutemann_-_Hannibals_%C3%9Cbergang_%C3%BCber_die_Alpen_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-Heinrich_Leutemann_-_Hannibals_%C3%9Cbergang_%C3%BCber_die_Alpen_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="302" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Heinrich_Leutemann_-_Hannibals_%C3%9Cbergang_%C3%BCber_die_Alpen_%28cropped%29.jpg/500px-Heinrich_Leutemann_-_Hannibals_%C3%9Cbergang_%C3%BCber_die_Alpen_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2140" data-file-height="2585"></a><figcaption>An 1866 illustration of Hannibal and his army crossing the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Leutemann" title="Heinrich Leutemann">Heinrich Leutemann</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Hannibal outmanoeuvred the natives who had tried to prevent his crossing, then evaded a Roman force marching from the Mediterranean coast by turning inland up the valley of the Rhône. His exact route over the Alps has been the source of scholarly dispute ever since (Polybius, the surviving ancient account closest in time to Hannibal's campaign, reports that the route was already debated). The most influential modern theories favour either a march up the valley of the <a href="/wiki/Dr%C3%B4me_(river)" title="Drôme (river)">Drôme</a> and a crossing of the main range to the south of the modern highway over the <a href="/wiki/Col_de_Montgen%C3%A8vre" title="Col de Montgenèvre">Col de Montgenèvre</a> or a march farther north up the valleys of the <a href="/wiki/Is%C3%A8re_(river)" title="Isère (river)">Isère</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arc_(Savoie)" title="Arc (Savoie)">Arc</a> crossing the main range near the present <a href="/wiki/Mont_Cenis" title="Mont Cenis">Col de Mont Cenis</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Little_St_Bernard_Pass" title="Little St Bernard Pass">Little St Bernard Pass</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Recent <a href="/wiki/Numismatics" title="Numismatics">numismatic</a> evidence suggests that Hannibal's army passed within sight of the <a href="/wiki/Matterhorn" title="Matterhorn">Matterhorn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Stanford geoarchaeologist Patrick Hunt argues that Hannibal took the <a href="/wiki/Col_de_Clapier" title="Col de Clapier">Col de Clapier</a> mountain pass, claiming the Clapier most accurately meets ancient depictions of the route: wide view of Italy, pockets of year-round snow, and a large campground.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Other scholars have doubts, proposing that Hannibal took the easier route across Petit <a href="/wiki/Mont_Cenis" title="Mont Cenis">Mount Cenis</a>. Hunt responds to this by proposing that Hannibal's Celtic guides purposefully misguided the Carthaginian general.
</p><p>Most recently, W. C. Mahaney has argued <a href="/wiki/Col_de_la_Traversette" title="Col de la Traversette">Col de la Traversette</a> closest fits the records of ancient authors.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Biostratigraphic archaeological data has reinforced the case for Col de la Traversette; analysis of peat bogs near watercourses on both sides of the pass's summit showed that the ground was heavily disturbed "by thousands, perhaps tens of thousands, of animals and humans" and that the soil bore traces of unique levels of <i><a href="/wiki/Clostridia" title="Clostridia">Clostridia</a></i> bacteria associated with the digestive tracts of horses and mules.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Radiocarbon dating secured dates of 2168 <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a> or c. 218 BC, the year of Hannibal's march. Mahaney <i>et al</i>. conclude that this and other evidence strongly supports the Col de la Traversette as being the "Hannibalic Route" as had been argued by <a href="/wiki/Gavin_de_Beer" title="Gavin de Beer">Gavin de Beer</a> in 1954. De Beer was one of only three interpreters—the others being John Lazenby and Jakob Seibert—to have visited all the Alpine high passes and presented a view on which was most plausible. Both De Beer and Seibert had selected the Col de la Traversette as the one most closely matching the ancient descriptions.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Polybius writes that Hannibal had crossed the highest of the Alpine passes: Col de la Traversette, between the upper <a href="/wiki/Guil" title="Guil">Guil</a> valley and the upper Po river, is the highest pass. It is moreover the most southerly, as Roman general <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Terentius_Varro" title="Gaius Terentius Varro">Varro</a> in his <i>De re rustica</i> relates, agreeing that Hannibal's passage was the highest in the western Alps and the most southerly. Mahaney <i>et al</i>. argue that factors used by De Beer to support Col de la Traversette including "gauging ancient place names against modern, close scrutiny of times of flood in major rivers and distant viewing of the Po plains" taken together with "massive radiocarbon and microbiological and parasitical evidence" from the alluvial sediments on either side of the pass furnish "supporting evidence, proof if you will" that Hannibal's invasion went that way.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> If Hannibal had ascended the Col de la Traversette, the Po Valley would have been visible from the pass's summit, vindicating Polybius's account.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>By Livy's account, the crossing was accomplished in the face of huge difficulties.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> These Hannibal surmounted with ingenuity, such as when he used <a href="/wiki/Fire-setting" title="Fire-setting">vinegar and fire</a> to break through a rockfall.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> According to Polybius, he arrived in Italy accompanied by 20,000 foot soldiers, 4,000 horsemen, and only a few elephants. The fired rockfall event is mentioned only by Livy; Polybius is mute on the subject and there is no evidence<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> of carbonized rock at the only two-tier rockfall in the western Alps, located below the Col de la Traversette (Mahaney, 2008). If Polybius is correct in his figure for the number of troops that he commanded after the crossing of the Rhône, this would suggest that he had lost almost half of his force. Historians such as Serge Lancel have questioned the reliability of the figures for the number of troops that he had when he left Hispania.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> From the start, he seems to have calculated that he would have to operate without aid from Hispania. 
</p><p>Hannibal's vision of military affairs was derived partly from the teaching of his Greek tutors and partly from experience gained alongside his father, and it stretched over most of the Hellenistic world of his time. The breadth of his vision gave rise to his grand strategy of conquering Rome by opening a northern front and subduing allied city-states on the peninsula, rather than by attacking Rome directly. Historical events that led to the defeat of Carthage during the First Punic War when his father commanded the Carthaginian Army also led Hannibal to plan the invasion of Italy by land across the Alps. The task involved the mobilization of between 60,000 and 100,000 troops and the training of a <a href="/wiki/War_elephant" title="War elephant">war elephant</a> corps, all of which had to be provisioned along the way. The alpine invasion of Italy was a military operation that would shake the Mediterranean world of 218&nbsp;BC with repercussions for more than two decades.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Battle_of_Trebia">Battle of Trebia</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Battle of Trebia"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Trebia" title="Battle of the Trebia">Battle of the Trebia</a></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_Trebia-numbers.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Battle_Trebia-numbers.svg/330px-Battle_Trebia-numbers.svg.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="250" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Battle_Trebia-numbers.svg/960px-Battle_Trebia-numbers.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="999" data-file-height="755"></a><figcaption>A diagram depicting the tactics used in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Trebia" title="Battle of the Trebia">Battle of the Trebia</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Hannibal's perilous march brought him into the Roman territory and frustrated the attempts of the enemy to fight out the main issue on foreign ground. His sudden appearance among the Gauls of the Po Valley enabled him to detach those tribes from their allegiance to the Romans before the Romans could take steps to check the rebellion. <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">Consul</a> <a href="/wiki/Publius_Cornelius_Scipio_(consul_218_BC)" title="Publius Cornelius Scipio (consul 218 BC)">Publius Cornelius Scipio</a>, the father of <a href="/wiki/Scipio_Africanus" title="Scipio Africanus">Scipio Africanus</a>, assumed responsibility for dealing with Hannibal.<sup id="cite_ref-dodge_old_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dodge_old-48"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Scipio had not expected Hannibal to make an attempt to cross the Alps and had already sent most of his forces abroad to fight the war in the Iberian Peninsula. With only a small detachment still positioned in Gaul, Scipio nevertheless attempted to confront Hannibal. He succeeded, through prompt decision and speedy movement, in transporting his army to Italy by sea in time to meet Hannibal. Hannibal's forces moved through the Po Valley and were engaged in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ticinus" title="Battle of Ticinus">Battle of Ticinus</a> in November 218 BC. Here, Hannibal forced the Romans to evacuate the plain of <a href="/wiki/Lombardy" title="Lombardy">Lombardy</a>, by virtue of his superior cavalry.<sup id="cite_ref-dodge_old_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dodge_old-48"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The victory was minor, but it encouraged the Gauls and <a href="/wiki/Ligures" title="Ligures">Ligurians</a> to join the Carthaginian cause. Their troops bolstered his army back to around 40,000 men. Scipio was severely injured, his life only saved by the bravery of his son who rode back onto the field to rescue his fallen father. Scipio retreated across the <a href="/wiki/Trebbia" title="Trebbia">Trebbia</a> to camp at <a href="/wiki/Piacenza" title="Piacenza">Placentia</a> with his army mostly intact.<sup id="cite_ref-dodge_old_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dodge_old-48"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Even before news of the defeat at Ticinus had reached Rome, the Senate had ordered Consul <a href="/wiki/Tiberius_Sempronius_Longus_(consul_218_BC)" title="Tiberius Sempronius Longus (consul 218 BC)">Tiberius Sempronius Longus</a> to bring his army back from Sicily to meet Scipio and face Hannibal. Hannibal, by skillful maneuvers, was in position to head him off, for he lay on the direct road between Placentia and Arminum, by which Sempronius would have to march to reinforce Scipio. Hannibal captured <a href="/wiki/Clastidium" title="Clastidium">Clastidium</a>, from which he drew large amounts of supplies for his men. But this gain was not without loss, as Sempronius avoided Hannibal's watchfulness, slipped around his flank, and joined his colleague near the Trebbia near Placentia. There, Hannibal had an opportunity to show his masterful military skill at the Trebia in December, wearing down the superior Roman infantry before cutting it to pieces with a surprise attack and <a href="/wiki/Flanking_maneuver" title="Flanking maneuver">ambush from the flanks</a>. However, most or all of his war elephants had died of injuries or the cold that winter, and none took part in the succeeding battles.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Battle_of_Lake_Trasimene">Battle of Lake Trasimene</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Battle of Lake Trasimene"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lake_Trasimene" title="Battle of Lake Trasimene">Battle of Lake Trasimene</a></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_lake_trasimene.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Battle_of_lake_trasimene.gif/330px-Battle_of_lake_trasimene.gif" decoding="async" width="330" height="253" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Battle_of_lake_trasimene.gif/960px-Battle_of_lake_trasimene.gif 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="983"></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lake_Trasimene" title="Battle of Lake Trasimene">Battle of Lake Trasimene</a>, 217&nbsp;BC.<br>From the Department of History, United States Military Academy</figcaption></figure>
<p>Hannibal quartered his troops for the winter with the Gauls, whose support for him had abated. Fearing the possibility that the tribesmen would seek to assassinate him and win back the Romans' favor, Hannibal resorted to altering his appearance by wearing differently dyed wigs in the hopes they would not recognize him.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-1"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In the spring of 217&nbsp;BC Hannibal decided to find a more reliable base of operations farther south. <a href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Servilius_Geminus" title="Gnaeus Servilius Geminus">Gnaeus Servilius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Flaminius_(consul_223_BC)" title="Gaius Flaminius (consul 223 BC)">Gaius Flaminius</a> (the new consuls of Rome) were expecting Hannibal to advance on Rome, and they took their armies to block the eastern and western routes that Hannibal could use.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The only alternative route to central Italy lay at the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Arno" title="Arno">Arno</a>. This area was mostly marshland and happened to be overflowing more than usual that spring. Hannibal knew that this route was full of difficulties, but it remained the surest and quickest way to central Italy. Polybius claims that Hannibal's men marched for four days and three nights "through a land that was under water", suffering terribly from fatigue and enforced want of sleep. He crossed without opposition over both the <a href="/wiki/Apennine_Mountains" title="Apennine Mountains">Apennines</a> (during which he lost his right eye<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> because of <a href="/wiki/Conjunctivitis" title="Conjunctivitis">conjunctivitis</a>) and the seemingly impassable Arno, but he lost a large part of his force in the marshy lowlands of the Arno.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> He arrived in <a href="/wiki/Etruria" title="Etruria">Etruria</a> in the spring of 217&nbsp;BC and decided to lure the main Roman army under Flaminius into a <a href="/wiki/Pitched_battle" title="Pitched battle">pitched battle</a> by devastating the region that Flaminius had been sent to protect. As Polybius recounts, "he [Hannibal] calculated that, if he passed the camp and made a descent into the district beyond, Flaminius (partly for fear of popular reproach and partly of personal irritation) would be unable to endure watching passively the devastation of the country but would spontaneously follow him... and give him opportunities for attack."<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>At the same time, Hannibal tried to break the allegiance of Rome's allies by proving that Flaminius was powerless to protect them. Despite this, Flaminius remained passively encamped at Arretium. Hannibal marched boldly around Flaminius' left flank, unable to draw him into battle by mere devastation, and effectively cut him off from Rome, executing the first recorded <a href="/wiki/Turning_movement" title="Turning movement">turning movement</a> in military history. He then advanced through the uplands of Etruria, provoking Flaminius into a hasty pursuit and catching him in a <a href="/wiki/Defile_(geography)" title="Defile (geography)">defile</a> on the shore of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Trasimeno" title="Lake Trasimeno">Lake Trasimenus</a>. There Hannibal destroyed Flaminius' army in the waters or on the adjoining slopes, killing Flaminius as well. This was the most costly ambush that the Romans ever sustained until the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carrhae" title="Battle of Carrhae">Battle of Carrhae</a> in 53 BC. Hannibal had disposed of the only field force that could check his advance upon Rome. He realized that without <a href="/wiki/Siege_engine" title="Siege engine">siege engines</a>, he could not hope to take the capital. He opted to exploit his victory by entering into central and southern Italy and encouraging a general revolt against the sovereign power.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The Romans appointed <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Fabius_Maximus_Verrucosus" title="Quintus Fabius Maximus Verrucosus">Quintus Fabius Maximus Verrucosus</a> as their dictator. Departing from Roman military traditions, Fabius adopted the <a href="/wiki/Fabian_strategy" title="Fabian strategy">strategy named after him</a>, avoiding open battle while placing several Roman armies in Hannibal's vicinity in order to watch and limit his movements. Hannibal ravaged <a href="/wiki/Apulia" title="Apulia">Apulia</a> but was unable to bring Fabius to battle, so he decided to march through <a href="/wiki/Samnium" title="Samnium">Samnium</a> to <a href="/wiki/Campania" title="Campania">Campania</a>, one of the richest and most fertile provinces of Italy, hoping that the devastation would draw Fabius into battle. Fabius closely followed Hannibal's path of destruction yet still refused to let himself be drawn out of the defensive. This strategy was unpopular with many Romans, who believed that it was a form of cowardice.
</p><p>Hannibal decided that it would be unwise to winter in the devastated lowlands of Campania, but Fabius had trapped him there by ensuring that all the exit passes were blocked. This situation led to the night <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ager_Falernus" title="Battle of Ager Falernus">Battle of Ager Falernus</a>. Hannibal had his men tie burning torches to the horns of a herd of cattle and drive them up the heights nearby. Some of the Romans, seeing a moving column of lights, were tricked into believing it was the Carthaginian army marching to escape along the heights. As they moved off in pursuit of this decoy, Hannibal managed to move his army in silence through the dark lowlands and up to an unguarded pass. Fabius was within striking distance, but in this case his caution worked against him, as rightly sensing a trick he stayed put. Thus, Hannibal managed to stealthily escape with his entire army intact. What Hannibal achieved in extricating his army was, as historian <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Goldsworthy" title="Adrian Goldsworthy">Adrian Goldsworthy</a> puts it, "a classic of ancient generalship, finding its way into nearly every historical narrative of the war and being used by later military manuals".<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> This was a severe blow to Fabius' prestige, and when his dictatorial term ended soon after, it was not renewed. For the winter, Hannibal found comfortable quarters in the Apulian plain.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Battle_of_Cannae">Battle of Cannae</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Battle of Cannae"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cannae" title="Battle of Cannae">Battle of Cannae</a></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_cannae_destruction.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Battle_cannae_destruction.svg/330px-Battle_cannae_destruction.svg.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="254" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Battle_cannae_destruction.svg/960px-Battle_cannae_destruction.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="737" data-file-height="567"></a><figcaption>The destruction of the Roman army (red) <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cannae" title="Battle of Cannae">at Cannae</a>, courtesy of the Department of History, United States Military Academy</figcaption></figure>
<p>In the spring of 216 BC Hannibal took the initiative and seized the large supply depot at <a href="/wiki/Cannae" title="Cannae">Cannae</a> in the Apulian plain. By capturing Cannae, Hannibal had placed himself between the Romans and their crucial sources of supply.<sup id="cite_ref-polybius_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-polybius-55"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Once the Roman Senate resumed their consular elections in 216 BC, they appointed <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Terentius_Varro" title="Gaius Terentius Varro">Gaius Terentius Varro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Aemilius_Paullus_(consul_219_BC)" title="Lucius Aemilius Paullus (consul 219 BC)">Lucius Aemilius Paullus</a> as consuls. In the meantime, the Romans hoped to gain success through sheer strength and weight of numbers, and they raised an army of unprecedented size, estimated by some to be as large as 100,000 men but more likely around 50,000–80,000.<sup id="cite_ref-cottrell_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cottrell-56"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The Romans and allied legions resolved to confront Hannibal and marched southward to Apulia. They eventually found him on the left bank of the <a href="/wiki/Ofanto" title="Ofanto">Aufidus River</a> and encamped 10&nbsp;km (6&nbsp;mi) away. On this occasion, the two armies were combined into one, the consuls having to alternate their command on a daily basis. According to Livy, Varro was a man of reckless and hubristic nature, and it was his turn to command on the day of battle. This account is possibly biased against Varro as its main source, Polybius, was a client of Paullus's aristocratic family whereas Varro was less distinguished. Some historians have suggested that the sheer size of the army required both generals to command a wing each. This theory is supported by the fact that after Varro survived the battle he was pardoned by the Senate, which would be peculiar if he were the sole commander at fault.<sup id="cite_ref-cottrell_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cottrell-56"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Hannibal capitalized on the eagerness of the Romans and drew them into a trap by using an <a href="/wiki/Pincer_movement" title="Pincer movement">envelopment tactic</a>. This eliminated the Roman numerical advantage by shrinking the combat area. Hannibal drew up his least reliable infantry in the centre in a semicircle curving towards the Romans. Placing them forward of the wings allowed them room to fall back, luring the Romans after them, while the cavalry on the flanks dealt with their Roman counterparts. Hannibal's wings were composed of the Gallic and Numidian cavalry.<sup id="cite_ref-cottrell_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cottrell-56"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The Roman legions forced their way through Hannibal's weak centre, but the Libyan mercenaries on the wings—swung around by the movement—menaced their flanks.
</p><p>The onslaught of Hannibal's cavalry was unstoppable. Hannibal's chief cavalry commander <a href="/wiki/Maharbal" title="Maharbal">Maharbal</a> led the mobile <a href="/wiki/Numidian_cavalry" title="Numidian cavalry">Numidian cavalry</a> on the right which shattered the Roman cavalry opposing them. Hannibal's Iberian and Gallic <a href="/wiki/Heavy_cavalry" title="Heavy cavalry">heavy cavalry</a> on the left, led by Hanno, defeated the Roman heavy cavalry after which both the Carthaginian heavy cavalry and the Numidians attacked the legions from behind. As a result, the Roman army was fully surrounded with no means of escape.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hannibal_Slodtz_Louvre_MR2093.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Hannibal_Slodtz_Louvre_MR2093.jpg/250px-Hannibal_Slodtz_Louvre_MR2093.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="415" style="--mw-file-upright: 0.75" class="mw-file-element mw-file-upright" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Hannibal_Slodtz_Louvre_MR2093.jpg/500px-Hannibal_Slodtz_Louvre_MR2093.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2500" data-file-height="4150"></a><figcaption>Hannibal counting the rings of the <a href="/wiki/Equites" title="Equites">Roman Knights</a> killed during the Battle of Cannae, statue by <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A9bastien_Slodtz" title="Sébastien Slodtz">Sébastien Slodtz</a>, 1704, <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>With these brilliant tactics Hannibal managed to surround and destroy all but a small remnant of his enemy, despite his inferior numbers. Depending upon the source, it is estimated that 50,000–70,000 Romans were killed or captured.<sup id="cite_ref-dodge_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dodge-14"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Among the dead were Roman Consul <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Aemilius_Paullus_(consul_219_BC)" title="Lucius Aemilius Paullus (consul 219 BC)">Lucius Aemilius Paullus</a>, two consuls for the preceding year, two <a href="/wiki/Quaestor" title="Quaestor">quaestors</a>, 29 of the 48 <a href="/wiki/Military_tribune" title="Military tribune">military tribunes</a>, and an additional 80 senators. At a time when the Roman Senate was composed of no more than 300 men, this constituted 25–30% of the governing body. This makes the battle one of the most catastrophic defeats in the history of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Rome</a> and one of the bloodiest battles in all of human history, in terms of the number of lives lost in a single day.<sup id="cite_ref-cottrell_56-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cottrell-56"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>After Cannae, the Romans were very hesitant to confront Hannibal in pitched battle, preferring instead to weaken him by <a href="/wiki/Attrition_warfare" title="Attrition warfare">attrition</a>, relying on their advantages of interior lines, supply, and manpower. As a result, Hannibal fought no more major battles in Italy for the rest of the war. It is believed that his refusal to bring the war to Rome was a lack of commitment from Carthage of men, money, and material—principally siege equipment. Whatever the reason, the choice prompted Maharbal to say, "Hannibal, you know how to gain a victory, but not how to use one."<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>As a result of this victory, many parts of Italy joined Hannibal's cause.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> As Polybius notes, "How much more serious was the defeat of Cannae, than those that preceded it can be seen by the behaviour of Rome's allies; before that fateful day, their loyalty remained unshaken, now it began to waver for the simple reason that they despaired of Roman Power."<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> During 216 BC the Greek cities in Sicily were induced to revolt against Roman political control, while King <a href="/wiki/Philip_V_of_Macedon" title="Philip V of Macedon">Philip V of Macedon</a> <a href="/wiki/Macedonian%E2%80%93Carthaginian_Treaty" title="Macedonian–Carthaginian Treaty">pledged his support</a> to Hannibal, initiating the <a href="/wiki/First_Macedonian_War" title="First Macedonian War">First Macedonian War</a> against Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-60"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>Hannibal also secured an alliance with newly appointed tyrant <a href="/wiki/Hieronymus_of_Syracuse" title="Hieronymus of Syracuse">Hieronymus of Syracuse</a>. It is often argued that if Hannibal had received proper material reinforcements from Carthage, he might have succeeded with a direct attack upon Rome. Instead, he had to content himself with subduing the fortresses that still held out against him, and the only other notable event of 216&nbsp;BC was the defection of certain Italian territories, including <a href="/wiki/Capua" title="Capua">Capua</a>, the second largest city of Italy, which Hannibal made his base. However, only a few of the Italian city-states that he had expected to gain as allies defected to him.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Stalemate">Stalemate</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Stalemate"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The war in Italy settled into a strategic stalemate. The Romans used the attritional strategy that Fabius had taught them, which they finally realized was the only feasible means of defeating Hannibal.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Fabius received the name "Cunctator" ("the Delayer") because of his policy of not meeting Hannibal in open battle.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The Romans deprived Hannibal of a large-scale battle and instead assaulted his weakening army with multiple smaller armies in an attempt to both weary him and create unrest in his troops.<sup id="cite_ref-dodge_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dodge-14"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> For the next few years, Hannibal was forced to sustain a <a href="/wiki/Scorched_earth" title="Scorched earth">scorched earth</a> policy and obtain local provisions for protracted and ineffectual operations throughout southern Italy. His immediate objectives were reduced to minor operations centred mainly around the cities of <a href="/wiki/Campania" title="Campania">Campania</a>.
</p><p>The forces detached to his lieutenants were generally unable to hold their own, and neither his home government nor Philip V helped to make up his losses. His position in southern Italy therefore became increasingly difficult, and his chance of ultimately conquering Rome grew ever more remote. Hannibal still won a number of notable victories: completely destroying two Roman armies in 212&nbsp;BC, and slaying two consuls including the famed <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Claudius_Marcellus" title="Marcus Claudius Marcellus">Marcus Claudius Marcellus</a> in a battle in 208&nbsp;BC. However, Hannibal slowly began losing ground—inadequately supported by his Italian allies, abandoned by his government (either because of jealousy or simply because Carthage was overstretched), and unable to match Rome's resources. He was not able to bring about another grand decisive victory that could produce a lasting strategic change.
</p><p>Carthaginian political will was embodied in the ruling <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchy</a>. There was a Carthaginian Senate, but the real power was with the inner "Council of 30 Nobles" and the board of judges from ruling families known as the "<a href="/wiki/Hundred_and_Four" title="Hundred and Four">Hundred and Four</a>". Two political factions operated in Carthage: the war party, also known as the "Barcids" (Hannibal's family name), and the peace party led by <a href="/wiki/Hanno_II_the_Great" title="Hanno II the Great">Hanno II the Great</a>. Hanno had been instrumental in denying Hannibal's requested reinforcements following the Battle of Cannae. He and his allies relied on trade with Rome to sustain their wealth and resented how Hannibal's unauthorized attack of Saguntum had forced them to choose between losing prestige or committing Carthage to war. Thus, they repeatedly acted to thwart Hannibal's plans; requests for supplies and equipment that would have made besieging Rome and its fortified cities possible were either delayed or ignored, and Carthaginian soldiers in northern Africa were not sent to reinforce Hannibal, forcing him to increasingly rely on local mercenaries who refused to be disciplined and whose loyalty lasted only until there was no plunder for them to earn.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Retreat">Retreat</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Retreat"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:ScipioAfricanusSulla.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/ScipioAfricanusSulla.jpg/250px-ScipioAfricanusSulla.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="318" style="--mw-file-upright: .8" class="mw-file-element mw-file-upright" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/ScipioAfricanusSulla.jpg/500px-ScipioAfricanusSulla.jpg 2x" data-file-width="569" data-file-height="723"></a><figcaption>A bust of doubtful provenance, possibly of <a href="/wiki/Scipio_Africanus" title="Scipio Africanus">Scipio Africanus</a>, and originally from the <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_the_Scipios" title="Tomb of the Scipios">Tomb of the Scipios</a></figcaption></figure>
