Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.<br><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Hannibal%22">"Hannibal"</a>&nbsp;–&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Hannibal%22+-wikipedia&amp;tbs=ar:1">news</a>&nbsp;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Hannibal%22&amp;tbs=bkt:s&amp;tbm=bks">newspapers</a>&nbsp;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Hannibal%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&nbsp;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Hannibal%22">scholar</a>&nbsp;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Hannibal%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2025</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In March 212&nbsp;BC Hannibal <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tarentum_(212_BC)" title="Battle of Tarentum (212 BC)">captured Tarentum</a> in a surprise attack, but he failed to obtain control of its harbor. The tide was slowly turning against him and in favor of Rome. The Roman consuls mounted a <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Capua_(212_BC)" title="Battle of Capua (212 BC)">siege of Capua</a> in 212 BC. Hannibal attacked them, forcing their withdrawal from Campania. He moved to Lucania and destroyed a 16,000-man Roman army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Silarus" title="Battle of the Silarus">Battle of the Silarus</a>, with 15,000 Romans killed. Another opportunity presented itself soon after, a Roman army of 18,000 men being destroyed by Hannibal at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Herdonia_(212_BC)" title="Battle of Herdonia (212 BC)">first battle of Herdonia</a> with 16,000 Romans dead, freeing Apulia from the Romans for the year. 
</p><p>The Roman consuls mounted another <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Capua_(211_BC)" title="Siege of Capua (211 BC)">siege of Capua</a> in 211&nbsp;BC, conquering the city. Hannibal's attempt to lift the siege with an assault on the Roman siege lines failed. He marched on Rome to force the recall of the Roman armies. He drew off 15,000 Roman soldiers, but the siege continued, and Capua fell. In 212 BC Marcellus <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Syracuse_(213%E2%80%93212_BC)" title="Siege of Syracuse (213–212 BC)">conquered Syracuse</a>, and the Romans destroyed the Carthaginian army in Sicily in 211–210 BC. In 210 BC, the Romans entered into an alliance with the <a href="/wiki/Aetolian_League" title="Aetolian League">Aetolian League</a> to counter Philip V. Philip, who attempted to exploit Rome's preoccupation in Italy to conquer <a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">Illyria</a>, found himself under attack from several sides at once and was quickly subdued by Rome and her Greek allies.
</p><p>In 210&nbsp;BC Hannibal again proved his superiority in tactics by inflicting a severe defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Herdonia_(210_BC)" title="Battle of Herdonia (210 BC)">Battle of Herdonia</a> in Apulia upon a <a href="/wiki/Proconsul" title="Proconsul">proconsular</a> army and, in 208&nbsp;BC he destroyed a Roman force engaged in the siege of <a href="/wiki/Locri" title="Locri">Locri</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Petelia" title="Battle of Petelia">Battle of Petelia</a>. But with the loss of Tarentum in 209&nbsp;BC and the gradual reconquest by the Romans of <a href="/wiki/Samnium" title="Samnium">Samnium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lucania" title="Lucania">Lucania</a>, his hold on south Italy was almost lost. In 207&nbsp;BC he succeeded in making his way again into Apulia, where he waited to concert measures for a combined march upon Rome with his brother Hasdrubal. On hearing of Hasdrubal's defeat and death at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Metaurus" title="Battle of the Metaurus">Battle of the Metaurus</a> he retired to <a href="/wiki/Calabria" title="Calabria">Calabria</a>, where he maintained himself for the ensuing years. Hasdrubal's head had been cut off, carried across Italy, and tossed over the palisade of Hannibal's camp as a cold message of the iron-clad will of the Roman Republic. The combination of these events marked the end to Hannibal's success in Italy. With the failure of his brother Mago in <a href="/wiki/Liguria" title="Liguria">Liguria</a> (205–203&nbsp;BC) and of his own negotiations with Phillip, the last hope of recovering his ascendancy in Italy was lost.  
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Conclusion_of_the_Second_Punic_War"></span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Conclusion_of_the_Second_Punic_War_(203–201_BC)"><span id="Conclusion_of_the_Second_Punic_War_.28203.E2.80.93201_BC.29"></span>Conclusion of the Second Punic War (203–201 BC)</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Conclusion of the Second Punic War (203–201 BC)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Return"></span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Return_to_Carthage">Return to Carthage</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Return to Carthage"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Campagna_africana_di_Scipione_202_aC.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Campagna_africana_di_Scipione_202_aC.png/500px-Campagna_africana_di_Scipione_202_aC.png" decoding="async" width="500" height="255" style="--mw-file-upright: 2.0" class="mw-file-element mw-file-upright" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Campagna_africana_di_Scipione_202_aC.png/1280px-Campagna_africana_di_Scipione_202_aC.png 2x" data-file-width="1530" data-file-height="780"></a><figcaption>The final act of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Punic_War" title="Second Punic War">Second Punic War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Zama" title="Battle of Zama">battle of Zama</a> (202 BC)</figcaption></figure>
<p>In 203&nbsp;BC, after nearly 15 years of fighting in Italy and with the military fortunes of Carthage rapidly declining, Hannibal was recalled to Carthage to direct the defense of his native country against a Roman invasion under Scipio Africanus. After leaving a record of his expedition engraved in <a href="/wiki/Punic_language" title="Punic language">Punic</a> and Greek upon bronze tablets in the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Juno_Lacinia_(Crotone)" title="Temple of Juno Lacinia (Crotone)">Temple of Juno Lacinia</a> at <a href="/wiki/Crotone" title="Crotone">Crotona</a>, he sailed back to Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> His arrival immediately restored the predominance of the war party, which placed him in command of a combined force of African <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">levies</a> and his mercenaries from Italy. In 202&nbsp;BC Hannibal met Scipio in a fruitless peace conference. 
</p><p>Despite mutual admiration, negotiations floundered due to Roman allegations of "Punic Faith," referring to the breach of protocols that ended the First Punic War by the Carthaginian attack on Saguntum and an attack on a stranded Roman fleet. Scipio and Carthage had worked out a peace plan, which was approved by Rome. The terms of the treaty were quite modest, but the war had been long for the Romans. Carthage could keep its African territory but would lose its overseas empire. Numidia was to be independent. Also, Carthage was to reduce its fleet and pay a war indemnity. Carthage then made a terrible blunder. Its long-suffering citizens had captured a stranded Roman fleet in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Tunis" title="Gulf of Tunis">Gulf of Tunis</a> and stripped it of supplies, an action that aggravated the faltering negotiations. Fortified by both Hannibal and the supplies, the Carthaginians rebuffed the treaty and Roman protests.  
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Battle_of_Zama_(202_BC)"><span id="Battle_of_Zama_.28202_BC.29"></span>Battle of Zama (202 BC)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Battle of Zama (202 BC)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Zama" title="Battle of Zama">Battle of Zama</a></div>
<p>Unlike most battles of the Second Punic War, at Zama the Romans were superior in cavalry and the Carthaginians had the edge in infantry. This Roman cavalry superiority was due to the betrayal of King <a href="/wiki/Masinissa" title="Masinissa">Masinissa</a> of Numidia, who had earlier assisted Carthage in Iberia but changed sides in 206&nbsp;BC with the promise of land, and due to his personal conflicts with <a href="/wiki/Syphax" title="Syphax">Syphax</a>, a Carthaginian ally. Although the ageing Hannibal was suffering from mental exhaustion and deteriorating health after years of campaigning in Italy, the Carthaginians still had the advantage in numbers and were boosted by 80 war elephants.
</p>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Slaget_ved_Zama_-_Cornelis_Cort,_1567.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Slaget_ved_Zama_-_Cornelis_Cort%2C_1567.jpg/250px-Slaget_ved_Zama_-_Cornelis_Cort%2C_1567.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="193" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Slaget_ved_Zama_-_Cornelis_Cort%2C_1567.jpg/500px-Slaget_ved_Zama_-_Cornelis_Cort%2C_1567.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1236"></a><figcaption>Engraving of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Zama" title="Battle of Zama">Battle of Zama</a> by <a href="/wiki/Cornelis_Cort" title="Cornelis Cort">Cornelis Cort</a>, 1567. Note that <a href="/wiki/Asian_elephant" title="Asian elephant">Asian elephants</a> are illustrated rather than the very small <a href="/wiki/North_African_elephant" title="North African elephant">North African elephants</a> used by Carthage.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The Roman cavalry won an early victory by swiftly routing the Carthaginian cavalry. The Romans were also successful in limiting the effectiveness of the war elephants with tactics such as playing trumpets to frighten the elephants and cause them to run into the Carthaginian lines. Some historians say that the elephants routed the Carthaginian cavalry and not the Romans, whilst others suggest that it was actually a tactical retreat planned by Hannibal.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Whatever the truth, the battle remained closely fought. At one point, it seemed that Hannibal was on the verge of victory, but Scipio was able to rally his men. Scipio's cavalry attacked Hannibal's rear. This two-pronged attack caused the Carthaginian formation to collapse.
</p><p>With their foremost general defeated, the Carthaginians had no choice but to surrender. Carthage lost approximately 20,000 troops with an additional 15,000 wounded. The Romans suffered 2,500 casualties. The last major battle of the Second Punic War resulted in a loss of respect for Hannibal by his fellow Carthaginians. The conditions of defeat were such that Carthage could no longer battle for Mediterranean supremacy.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Later_career">Later career</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Later career"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Peacetime_Carthage"></span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Peacetime_Carthage_(200–196_BC)"><span id="Peacetime_Carthage_.28200.E2.80.93196_BC.29"></span>Peacetime Carthage (200–196 BC)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Peacetime Carthage (200–196 BC)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Buste_Hannibal_Barca.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Buste_Hannibal_Barca.jpg/250px-Buste_Hannibal_Barca.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="360" style="--mw-file-upright: 0.75" class="mw-file-element mw-file-upright" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Buste_Hannibal_Barca.jpg/500px-Buste_Hannibal_Barca.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2872" data-file-height="4136"></a><figcaption>A bust of Hannibal, Bardo National Museum, Tunisia</figcaption></figure>
<p>Hannibal was 46 at the conclusion of the Second Punic War in 201 BC and quickly showed that he could be a statesman as well as a soldier. Following the conclusion of a peace that left Carthage saddled with an indemnity of ten thousand <a href="/wiki/Attic_talent" title="Attic talent">talents</a>, he was elected <a href="/wiki/Shophet" title="Shophet">suffete</a> (chief magistrate) of the Carthaginian state.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated291_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated291-65"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> After an audit confirmed Carthage had the resources to pay the indemnity without increasing taxation, Hannibal initiated a reorganization of state finances aimed at eliminating corruption and recovering embezzled funds.<sup id="cite_ref-brainfly2_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brainfly2-66"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The principal beneficiaries of these financial peculations had been the oligarchs of the Hundred and Four.<sup id="cite_ref-brainfly2_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brainfly2-66"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In order to reduce their power, Hannibal passed a law stipulating the Hundred and Four be chosen by direct election rather than <a href="/wiki/Co-option" title="Co-option">co-option</a>. He also used citizen support to change the term of office in the Hundred and Four from life to one year, with none permitted to hold office for two consecutive years.<sup id="cite_ref-brainfly2_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brainfly2-66"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated291_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated291-65"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Exile"></span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Exile_(after_195_BC)"><span id="Exile_.28after_195_BC.29"></span>Exile (after 195 BC)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Exile (after 195 BC)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Seven years after the victory of Zama, the Romans, alarmed by Carthage's renewed prosperity and suspicious that Hannibal had been in contact with <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_III_the_Great" title="Antiochus III the Great">Antiochus III</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>, sent a delegation to Carthage alleging that Hannibal was helping an enemy of Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated296_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated296-67"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Aware that he had many enemies, not the least of which were because of his financial reforms eliminating corruption, Hannibal fled into voluntary exile before the Romans could demand that Carthage surrender him into their custody.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated296_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated296-67"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>He journeyed first to <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre</a>, the mother city of Carthage, and then to <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>, before he finally reached <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a> where he was honourably received by Antiochus. Livy states that Antiochus consulted Hannibal on the strategic concerns of making war on Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-brainfly2_66-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brainfly2-66"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>, while at the court of Antiochus, Hannibal attended a lecture by Phormio, a philosopher, that ranged through many topics. When Phormio finished a discourse on the duties of a general, Hannibal was asked his opinion. He replied, "I have seen during my life many old fools; but this one beats them all." Another story, according to <a href="/wiki/Aulus_Gellius" title="Aulus Gellius">Aulus Gellius</a>, is that after Antiochus showed Hannibal the gigantic and elaborately equipped army he had created to invade Greece, he asked him if they would be enough for the Roman Republic, to which Hannibal replied, "I think all this will be enough, yes, quite enough, for the Romans, even though they are most avaricious."<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the summer of 193 BC, tensions flared up between the Seleucids and Rome. Antiochus gave tacit support to Hannibal's plans of launching an anti-Roman coup d'état in Carthage, yet it was not carried out.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Hannibal advised equipping a fleet and landing a body of troops in the south of Italy, offering to take command himself.<sup id="cite_ref-brainfly2_66-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brainfly2-66"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In 190 BC, after having suffered a series of defeats in the <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Seleucid_war" title="Roman–Seleucid war">Roman–Seleucid war</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Antiochus gave Hannibal his first significant military command after spending five years in the Seleucid court.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Hannibal was tasked with building a fleet in <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a> from scratch. Although Phoenician territories like Tyre and <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a> possessed the necessary combination of raw materials, technical expertise, and experienced personnel, it took much longer than expected for it to be completed, most likely due to wartime shortages.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Artaxias_and_Hannibal_Barca.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Artaxias_and_Hannibal_Barca.png/250px-Artaxias_and_Hannibal_Barca.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="357" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Artaxias_and_Hannibal_Barca.png/500px-Artaxias_and_Hannibal_Barca.png 2x" data-file-width="2755" data-file-height="3928"></a><figcaption>Hannibal with <a href="/wiki/Artaxias_I" title="Artaxias I">Artaxias I</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Armenia_(antiquity)" title="Kingdom of Armenia (antiquity)">Greater Armenia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ayrarat" title="Ayrarat">Ayrarat</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>In July 190 BC Hannibal ordered his fleet to set sail from <a href="/wiki/Seleucia_Pieria" title="Seleucia Pieria">Seleucia Pieria</a> in order to reinforce the rest of the Seleucid navy at Ephesus.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The following month Hannibal's fleet clashed with the <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodian</a> navy in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Eurymedon_(190_BC)" title="Battle of the Eurymedon (190 BC)">Battle of Side</a>. The faster Rhodian ships managed to heavily damage half of Hannibal's warships through the <span title="Greek-language text"><i lang="el"><a href="/wiki/Diekplous" class="mw-redirect" title="Diekplous">diekplous</a></i></span> manoeuvre, forcing him to retreat.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Hannibal had preserved most of his fleet; however, he was in no position to unite with <a href="/wiki/Polyxenidas" title="Polyxenidas">Polyxenidas</a>' fleet at Ephesus since his ships required extensive repairs.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>The ensuing <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Myonessus" title="Battle of Myonessus">Battle of Myonessus</a> resulted in a Roman-Rhodian victory, which cemented Roman control over the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a>, enabling them to launch an invasion of Seleucid <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Asia Minor</a>. The two armies faced off in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Magnesia" title="Battle of Magnesia">Battle of Magnesia</a>, resulting in a decisive Roman-Pergamene victory.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The truce was signed at <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardes</a> in January 189 BC, whereupon Antiochus agreed to abandon his claims on all lands west of the <a href="/wiki/Taurus_Mountains" title="Taurus Mountains">Taurus Mountains</a>, paid a heavy war indemnity, and promised to hand over Hannibal and other notable enemies of Rome from among his allies.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>, Hannibal received hospitality at the Armenian royal court of <a href="/wiki/Artaxias_I" title="Artaxias I">Artaxias I</a>. The authors add an apocryphal story of how Hannibal planned and supervised the building of the new royal capital <a href="/wiki/Artaxata" title="Artaxata">Artaxata</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Suspicious that Antiochus was prepared to surrender him to the Romans, Hannibal fled to <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, but he soon went back to Anatolia and sought refuge with <a href="/wiki/Prusias_I_of_Bithynia" title="Prusias I of Bithynia">Prusias I of Bithynia</a>, who was engaged in warfare with Rome's ally, King <a href="/wiki/Eumenes_II" title="Eumenes II">Eumenes II</a> of Pergamon.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Hannibal went on to serve Prusias in this war. In 190 BC he gained a naval victory over Eumenes by throwing clay pots filled with venomous snakes onto Eumenes' ships.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In 184 BC Hannibal defeated Eumenes two more times in battles on land.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornelius_Nepos,_Hannibal_12_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornelius_Nepos,_Hannibal_12-81"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Death"></span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Death_(183–181_BC)"><span id="Death_.28183.E2.80.93181_BC.29"></span>Death (183–181 BC)</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Death (183–181 BC)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>At this stage, the Romans intervened and threatened <a href="/wiki/Bithynia" title="Bithynia">Bithynia</a> into giving up Hannibal.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornelius_Nepos,_Hannibal_12_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornelius_Nepos,_Hannibal_12-81"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Prusias_I_of_Bithynia" title="Prusias I of Bithynia">Prusias</a> agreed, but Hannibal was determined not to fall into his enemy's hands. The precise year and cause of Hannibal's death are unknown. Pausanias writes that Hannibal's death occurred after his finger was wounded by his drawn sword while mounting his horse, resulting in a fever and then his death three days later.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_Nepos" title="Cornelius Nepos">Cornelius Nepos</a><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and Livy<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> tell a different story, namely that the ex-consul <a href="/wiki/Titus_Quinctius_Flamininus" title="Titus Quinctius Flamininus">Titus Quinctius Flamininus</a>, on discovering that Hannibal was in Bithynia, went there in an embassy to demand his surrender from Prusias. Hannibal, discovering that the castle where he was living was surrounded by Roman soldiers and he could not escape, took poison. <a href="/wiki/Appian" title="Appian">Appian</a> writes that it was Prusias who poisoned Hannibal.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-85"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>, in his <i>Life of Flamininus</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> record that Hannibal's tomb was at <a href="/wiki/Libyssa" title="Libyssa">Libyssa</a> on the coast of the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Marmara" title="Sea of Marmara">Sea of Marmara</a>. According to some, Libyssa was sited at <a href="/wiki/Gebze" title="Gebze">Gebze</a>, between Bursa and Üskudar. W. M. Leake,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> identifying Gebze with ancient <a href="/wiki/Dacibyza" title="Dacibyza">Dakibyza</a>, places it further west. Before dying, Hannibal is said to have left behind a letter declaring, "Let us relieve the Romans from the anxiety they have so long experienced, since they think it tries their patience too much to wait for an old man's death".<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Appian writes of a prophecy about Hannibal's death, which states that "Libyssan earth shall cover Hannibal's remains." This, he writes, made Hannibal believe that he would die in Libya, but instead it was at the Bithynian Libyssa that he would die.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-85"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In his <i>Annales</i>, <a href="/wiki/Titus_Pomponius_Atticus" title="Titus Pomponius Atticus">Titus Pomponius Atticus</a> reports that Hannibal's death occurred in 183&nbsp;BC,<sup id="cite_ref-Cornelius_Nepos,_Hannibal_13.1_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornelius_Nepos,_Hannibal_13.1-90"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and Livy implies the same. Polybius, who wrote nearest the event, gives 182&nbsp;BC. Sulpicius Blitho<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> records the death under 181&nbsp;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Cornelius_Nepos,_Hannibal_13.1_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cornelius_Nepos,_Hannibal_13.1-90"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Legacy">Legacy</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Legacy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_West_(1738-1820)_-_The_Oath_of_Hannibal_-_RCIN_405417_-_Royal_Collection.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Benjamin_West_%281738-1820%29_-_The_Oath_of_Hannibal_-_RCIN_405417_-_Royal_Collection.jpg/250px-Benjamin_West_%281738-1820%29_-_The_Oath_of_Hannibal_-_RCIN_405417_-_Royal_Collection.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="180" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Benjamin_West_%281738-1820%29_-_The_Oath_of_Hannibal_-_RCIN_405417_-_Royal_Collection.jpg/500px-Benjamin_West_%281738-1820%29_-_The_Oath_of_Hannibal_-_RCIN_405417_-_Royal_Collection.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2250" data-file-height="1624"></a><figcaption><i><a href="/wiki/The_Oath_of_Hannibal" title="The Oath of Hannibal">The Oath of Hannibal</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_West" title="Benjamin West">Benjamin West</a>, 1770</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Ancient_world">Ancient world</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Ancient world"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Hannibal caused great distress to many in Roman society. He became such a figure of terror that whenever disaster threatened, Romans would exclaim "<i><a href="/wiki/List_of_Latin_phrases:_H#Hannibal_ad_portas" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Latin phrases: H">Hannibal ad portas</a></i>" ("Hannibal is at the gates!") to emphasize the gravity of the emergency, a phrase still used in modern languages.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>His legacy was recorded by his Greek tutor, <a href="/wiki/Sosylus_of_Lacedaemon" title="Sosylus of Lacedaemon">Sosylus of Lacedaemon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-60"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The works of Roman writers such as Livy (64 or 59 BC – AD 12 or 17), <a href="/wiki/Frontinus" title="Frontinus">Frontinus</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> AD 40–103), and <a href="/wiki/Juvenal" title="Juvenal">Juvenal</a> (1st–2nd century AD) show a grudging admiration for Hannibal. The Romans even built statues of him in the streets of Rome to advertise their defeat of such a worthy adversary.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> It is plausible to suggest that Hannibal engendered the greatest fear Rome had towards an enemy. Nevertheless, the Romans grimly refused to admit the possibility of defeat and rejected all overtures for peace; they even refused to accept the ransom of prisoners after the Battle of Cannae.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>During the war there are no reports of revolutions among the Roman citizens, no factions within the Senate desiring peace, no pro-Carthaginian Roman turncoats, and no coups.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Throughout the war, Roman aristocrats ferociously competed with each other for positions of command to fight against Rome's most dangerous enemy. Hannibal's military genius was not enough to really disturb the Roman political process and the collective political and military capacity of the Roman people. As Lazenby states,
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>It says volumes, too, for their political maturity and respect for constitutional forms that the complicated machinery of government continued to function even amidst disaster—there are few states in the ancient world in which a general who had lost a battle like Cannae would have dared to remain, let alone would have continued to be treated respectfully as head of state.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote><p>
According to Livy, the Romans feared Hannibal's military genius, and during Hannibal's march against Rome in 211&nbsp;BC:</p><blockquote><p>
a messenger who had travelled from Fregellae for a day and a night without stopping created great alarm in Rome, and the excitement was increased by people running about the City with wildly exaggerated accounts of the news he had brought. The wailing cry of the <a href="/wiki/Women_in_ancient_Rome" title="Women in ancient Rome">matrons</a> was heard everywhere, not only in private houses but even in the temples. Here they knelt and swept the temple-floors with their dishevelled hair and lifted up their hands to heaven in piteous entreaty to the gods that they would deliver the City of Rome out of the hands of the enemy and preserve its mothers and children from injury and outrage.<sup id="cite_ref-livy26_8_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livy26_8-99"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup></p></blockquote><p> In the Senate, the news was "received with varying feelings as men's temperaments differed",<sup id="cite_ref-livy26_8_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livy26_8-99"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> so it was decided to keep Capua under siege but to send 15,000 infantry and 1,000 cavalry as reinforcements to Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-livy26_8_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livy26_8-99"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to Livy, the land occupied by Hannibal's army outside Rome in 211&nbsp;BC was sold by a Roman while it was occupied.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> This may not be true, but as Lazenby states, "could well be, exemplifying as it does not only the supreme confidence felt by the Romans in ultimate victory, but also the way in which something like normal life continued."<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> After Cannae, the Romans showed considerable steadfastness. As an example of Rome's confidence, after the Cannae disaster she was left virtually defenseless; however, the Senate still chose not to withdraw a single garrison from an overseas province to strengthen the city. In fact, they were reinforced and the campaigns there maintained until victory was secured; beginning first in Sicily under the direction of Claudius Marcellus, and later in Hispania under Scipio Africanus.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Although the long-term consequences of Hannibal's war are debatable, this war was undeniably Rome's "finest hour".<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Most of the sources available to historians about Hannibal are from Romans. They considered him the greatest enemy Rome had ever faced. Livy indicates that Hannibal was extremely cruel. Even <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>, when he talked of Rome and its two great enemies, spoke of the "honourable" Pyrrhus and the "cruel" Hannibal. Yet a different picture sometimes emerges. When Hannibal's successes had brought about the death of two Roman consuls, he vainly searched for the body of Gaius Flaminius on the shores of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Trasimeno" title="Lake Trasimeno">Lake Trasimene</a>, held ceremonial rituals in recognition of Lucius Aemilius Paullus, and sent Marcellus' ashes back to his family in Rome. Any bias attributed to Polybius, however, is more troublesome. Historian <a href="/wiki/Ronald_J._Mellor" title="Ronald J. Mellor">Ronald Mellor</a> considers the Greek scholar a loyal partisan of <a href="/wiki/Scipio_Aemilianus" title="Scipio Aemilianus">Scipio Aemilianus</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> while H. Ormerod does not view him as an "altogether unprejudiced witness" when it came to his pet peeves, the Aetolians, the Carthaginians, and the Cretans.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Nonetheless, Polybius did recognize that the reputation for cruelty the Romans attached to Hannibal might in reality have been by mistaking him for one of his officers, Hannibal Monomachus.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Severan_dynasty" title="Severan dynasty">Severan period</a>, Hannibal was portrayed as a successful military leader from history who could serve as an exemplary figure for a Roman audience.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-109"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In the 13th century, Byzantine scholar <a href="/wiki/John_Tzetzes" title="John Tzetzes">John Tzetzes</a> writes that Severus (likely <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">Septimius Severus</a>), being of Libyan birth, constructed a tomb of white marble for Hannibal in Libyssa. Scholars debate whether this act was intended to promote a unified North African identity, stimulate local economic interests, or link Severus with past military heroes to strengthen his legacy, reflecting a broader Severan policy of honoring local traditions and historical figures.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-109"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Military_history">Military history</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Military history"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Mallord_William_Turner_081.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Joseph_Mallord_William_Turner_081.jpg/250px-Joseph_Mallord_William_Turner_081.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="153" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Joseph_Mallord_William_Turner_081.jpg/500px-Joseph_Mallord_William_Turner_081.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="942"></a><figcaption>The material of legend: in <i><a href="/wiki/Snow_Storm:_Hannibal_and_his_Army_Crossing_the_Alps" title="Snow Storm: Hannibal and his Army Crossing the Alps">Snow Storm: Hannibal and his Army Crossing the Alps</a></i> (1812)  <a href="/wiki/J._M._W._Turner" title="J. M. W. Turner">J. M. W. Turner</a> envelops Hannibal's crossing of the Alps in <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic</a> atmosphere.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Hannibal is generally regarded as one of the best military strategists and tacticians of all time, the double envelopment at Cannae an enduring legacy of tactical brilliance. Military academies all over the world continue to study Hannibal's exploits, especially his victory at Cannae.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Appian" title="Appian">Appian</a>, several years after the Second Punic War, Hannibal served as a political advisor in the Seleucid Kingdom and Scipio arrived there on a diplomatic mission from Rome.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It is said that at one of their meetings in the gymnasium Scipio and Hannibal had a conversation on the subject of generalship, in the presence of a number of bystanders, and that Scipio asked Hannibal whom he considered the greatest general, to which the latter replied "<a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander of Macedonia</a>".
</p><p>To this Scipio assented since he also yielded the first place to Alexander. Then he asked Hannibal whom he placed next, and he replied "<a href="/wiki/Pyrrhus_of_Epirus" title="Pyrrhus of Epirus">Pyrrhus of Epirus</a>", because he considered boldness the first qualification of a general; "for it would not be possible", he said, "to find two kings more enterprising than these".
</p><p>Scipio was rather nettled by this, but nevertheless he asked Hannibal to whom he would give the third place, expecting that at least the third would be assigned to him; but Hannibal replied, "to myself; for when I was a young man I conquered Hispania and crossed the Alps with an army, the first after <a href="/wiki/Hercules" title="Hercules">Hercules</a>."
</p><p>As Scipio saw that he was likely to prolong his self-laudation he said, laughing, "where would you place yourself, Hannibal, if you had not been defeated by me?" Hannibal, now perceiving his jealousy, replied, "in that case I should have put myself before Alexander". Thus Hannibal continued his self-laudation, but flattered Scipio in an indirect manner by suggesting that he had conquered one who was the superior of Alexander.
</p><p>
At the end of this conversation Hannibal invited Scipio to be his guest, and Scipio replied that he would be so gladly if Hannibal were not living with <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_III_the_Great" title="Antiochus III the Great">Antiochus</a>, who was held in suspicion by the Romans. Thus did they, in a manner worthy of great commanders, cast aside their enmity at the end of their wars.<sup id="cite_ref-appian_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-appian-111"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hannibal_in_Italy_by_Jacopo_Ripanda_-_Sala_di_Annibale_-_Palazzo_dei_Conservatori_-_Musei_Capitolini_-_Rome_2016_(2).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Hannibal_in_Italy_by_Jacopo_Ripanda_-_Sala_di_Annibale_-_Palazzo_dei_Conservatori_-_Musei_Capitolini_-_Rome_2016_%282%29.jpg/250px-Hannibal_in_Italy_by_Jacopo_Ripanda_-_Sala_di_Annibale_-_Palazzo_dei_Conservatori_-_Musei_Capitolini_-_Rome_2016_%282%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="176" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Hannibal_in_Italy_by_Jacopo_Ripanda_-_Sala_di_Annibale_-_Palazzo_dei_Conservatori_-_Musei_Capitolini_-_Rome_2016_%282%29.jpg/500px-Hannibal_in_Italy_by_Jacopo_Ripanda_-_Sala_di_Annibale_-_Palazzo_dei_Conservatori_-_Musei_Capitolini_-_Rome_2016_%282%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4912" data-file-height="3454"></a><figcaption> Hannibal's celebrated feat in crossing the Alps with <a href="/wiki/War_elephant" title="War elephant">war elephants</a> passed into European legend: detail of a fresco by <a href="/wiki/Jacopo_Ripanda" title="Jacopo Ripanda">Jacopo Ripanda</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1510</span>, <a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Museums" title="Capitoline Museums">Capitoline Museums</a>, Rome.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Maximilian Otto Bismarck Caspari in his article in the <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica_Eleventh_Edition" class="mw-redirect" title="Encyclopædia Britannica Eleventh Edition">Encyclopædia Britannica Eleventh Edition</a> (1910–1911), praises Hannibal in these words:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>As to the transcendent military genius of Hannibal there cannot be two opinions. The man who for fifteen years could hold his ground in a hostile country against several powerful armies and a succession of able generals must have been a commander and a tactician of supreme capacity. In the use of strategies and ambuscades he certainly surpassed all other generals of antiquity. Wonderful as his achievements were, we must marvel the more when we take into account the grudging support he received from Carthage. As his veterans melted away, he had to organize fresh levies on the spot. We never hear of a mutiny in his army, composed though it was of North Africans, Iberians and <a href="/wiki/Gauls" title="Gauls">Gauls</a>. Again, all we know of him comes for the most part from hostile sources. The Romans feared and hated him so much that they could not do him justice. Livy speaks of his great qualities, but he adds that his vices were equally great, among which he singles out his more than <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fides_Punica" class="extiw" title="wikt:fides Punica">Punic perfidy</a> and an inhuman cruelty. For the first there would seem to be no further justification than that he was consummately skillful in the use of ambuscades. For the latter there is, we believe, no more ground than that at certain crises he acted in the general spirit of ancient warfare. Sometimes he contrasts most favorably with his enemy. No such brutality stains his name as that perpetrated by <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Claudius_Nero" title="Gaius Claudius Nero">Gaius Claudius Nero</a> on the vanquished <a href="/wiki/Hasdrubal_Barca" title="Hasdrubal Barca">Hasdrubal</a>. Polybius merely says that he was accused of cruelty by the Romans and of avarice by the Carthaginians. He had indeed bitter enemies, and his life was one continuous struggle against destiny. For steadfastness of purpose, for organizing capacity and a mastery of military science he has perhaps never had an equal.<sup id="cite_ref-1911britannica_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1911britannica-112"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Even the Roman chroniclers acknowledged Hannibal's supreme military leadership, writing that "he never required others to do what he could not and would not do himself".<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> According to Polybius 23, 13, p.&nbsp;423:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It is a remarkable and very cogent proof of Hannibal's having been by nature a real leader and far superior to anyone else in statesmanship, that though he spent seventeen years in the field, passed through so many barbarous countries, and employed to aid him in desperate and extraordinary enterprises numbers of men of different nations and languages, no one ever dreamt of conspiring against him, nor was he ever deserted by those who had once joined him or submitted to him.</p></blockquote>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Museum_of_Antiquities_Hannibal.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Museum_of_Antiquities_Hannibal.JPG/250px-Museum_of_Antiquities_Hannibal.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="366" style="--mw-file-upright: 0.75" class="mw-file-element mw-file-upright" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Museum_of_Antiquities_Hannibal.JPG/500px-Museum_of_Antiquities_Hannibal.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2167" data-file-height="3169"></a><figcaption>A bust of Hannibal, 17th century, <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Antiquities_(Saskatoon)" title="Museum of Antiquities (Saskatoon)">Museum of Antiquities (Saskatoon)</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Prior to World War I, Count <a href="/wiki/Alfred_von_Schlieffen" title="Alfred von Schlieffen">Alfred von Schlieffen</a> developed his "<a href="/wiki/Schlieffen_Plan" title="Schlieffen Plan">Schlieffen Plan</a>" from his military studies, including the envelopment technique that Hannibal employed in the Battle of Cannae.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_S._Patton" title="George S. Patton">George S. Patton</a> believed himself a reincarnation of Hannibal—as well as of many other people, including a Roman <a href="/wiki/Legionary" title="Legionary">legionary</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic</a> soldier.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Norman_Schwarzkopf_Jr." title="Norman Schwarzkopf Jr.">Norman Schwarzkopf Jr.</a>, the commander of the <a href="/wiki/Coalition_of_the_Gulf_War" title="Coalition of the Gulf War">Coalition of the Gulf War</a> of 1990–1991, claimed, "The technology of war may change, the sophistication of weapons certainly changes. But those same principles of war that applied to the days of Hannibal apply today."<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to the military historian <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Ayrault_Dodge" title="Theodore Ayrault Dodge">Theodore Ayrault Dodge</a>,
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Hannibal excelled as a tactician. No battle in history is a finer sample of tactics than Cannae. But he was yet greater in logistics and strategy. No captain ever marched to and fro among so many armies of troops superior to his own numbers and material as fearlessly and skilfully as he. No man ever held his own so long or so ably against such odds. Constantly overmatched by better soldiers, led by generals always respectable, often of great ability, he yet defied all their efforts to drive him from Italy, for half a generation. Excepting in the case of Alexander, and some few isolated instances, all wars up to the Second Punic War, had been decided largely, if not entirely, by battle-tactics. Strategic ability had been comprehended only on a minor scale. Armies had marched towards each other, had fought in parallel order, and the conqueror had imposed terms on his opponent. Any variation from this rule consisted in ambuscades or other stratagems. That war could be waged by avoiding in lieu of seeking battle; that the results of a victory could be earned by attacks upon the enemy's communications, by flank-manoeuvres, by seizing positions from which safely to threaten him in case he moved, and by other devices of strategy, was not understood... [However,] for the first time in the history of war, we see two contending generals avoiding each other, occupying impregnable camps on heights, marching about each other's flanks to seize cities or supplies in their rear, harassing each other with <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla" class="mw-redirect" title="Guerrilla">small-war</a>, and rarely venturing on a battle which might prove a fatal disaster—all with a well-conceived purpose of placing his opponent at a strategic disadvantage... That it did so was due to the teaching of Hannibal.<sup id="cite_ref-dodge_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dodge-14"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="In_modern_Tunisia">In modern Tunisia</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: In modern Tunisia"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Due to his origin and connection with the territory belonging to modern-day Tunisia, he is widely revered as a national hero in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Hannibal's likeness is depicted on the <a href="/wiki/Tunisian_dinar" title="Tunisian dinar">Tunisian five-dinar bill</a> issued on 8 November 1993, as well as on a bill put into circulation on 20 March 2013. His name appears in the name of a private television channel, <a href="/wiki/Hannibal_TV" title="Hannibal TV">Hannibal TV</a>. A street in <a href="/wiki/Carthage_(municipality)" title="Carthage (municipality)">Carthage</a>, located near the Punic ports, bears his name; as does as a station on the <a href="/wiki/Tunis-Goulette-Marsa" title="Tunis-Goulette-Marsa">TGM</a> railway line: "Carthage Hannibal". Plans envisage a mausoleum and a 17-metre (56&nbsp;ft) high colossus of Hannibal on the <a href="/wiki/Byrsa" title="Byrsa">Byrsa</a>, the highest point of Carthage overlooking <a href="/wiki/Tunis" title="Tunis">Tunis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Other">Other</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:HAN%C4%B0BALIN_MEZARI-GEBZE_(2).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/HAN%C4%B0BALIN_MEZARI-GEBZE_%282%29.jpg/250px-HAN%C4%B0BALIN_MEZARI-GEBZE_%282%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/HAN%C4%B0BALIN_MEZARI-GEBZE_%282%29.jpg/500px-HAN%C4%B0BALIN_MEZARI-GEBZE_%282%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592"></a><figcaption>Hannibal's monumental tomb in <a href="/wiki/Kocaeli_Province" title="Kocaeli Province">Kocaeli</a>, Turkey</figcaption></figure>
<p>The teenaged <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a> regarded Hannibal as a "hero"; the founder of <a href="/wiki/Psychoanalysis" title="Psychoanalysis">psychoanalysis</a> portrays an idealized image of Hannibal in his analysis of his "dreams of Rome" in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Interpretation_of_Dreams" title="The Interpretation of Dreams">The Interpretation of Dreams</a></i>. Freud associates this phenomenon with the adage "All roads lead to Rome". He writes: "Hannibal and Rome symbolized for the adolescent that I was the opposition between the tenacity of Judaism and the organizing spirit of the Catholic Church".<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kocaeli_Province" title="Kocaeli Province">Kocaeli</a> in Turkey has a <a href="/wiki/Cenotaph" title="Cenotaph">cenotaph</a> built in Hannibal's memory. Even though the location of Hannibal's tomb could not be determined precisely in the studies carried out through President <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Kemal_Atat%C3%BCrk" title="Mustafa Kemal Atatürk">Atatürk's</a> great interest, a monumental cenotaph was built in 1981 in the south of present-day <a href="/wiki/Gebze" title="Gebze">Gebze</a> as an expression of Atatürk's respect for Hannibal.
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Citations">Citations</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hannibal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Citations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1327269900">.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}body.skin-vector-2022 .mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:27em}body.skin-vector-2022 .mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:22.5em}.mw-parser-output .references[data-mw-group=upper-alpha]{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .references[data-mw-group=upper-roman]{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .references[data-mw-group=lower-alpha]{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .references[data-mw-group=lower-greek]{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .references[data-mw-group=lower-roman]{list-style-type:lower-roman}.mw-parser-output div.reflist-liststyle-upper-alpha .references{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output div.reflist-liststyle-upper-roman .references{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output div.reflist-liststyle-lower-alpha .references{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output div.reflist-liststyle-lower-greek .references{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output div.reflist-liststyle-lower-roman .references{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div>
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-:1-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-:1_1-0"><span class="cite-accessibility-label">Jump up to: </span><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:1_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Plutarch, <i>Life of Titus Flamininus</i> 21.3–4. Plutarch adds that "when asked what his choices would be if he had beaten Scipio, he replied that he would be the best of them all". However, Plutarch gives another version in his <i>Life of Pyrrhus</i>, 8.2: "Pyrrhus, Scipio, then myself".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuss1985[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidNvEK7kc3qnQCpgPA565_565]-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuss1985[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidNvEK7kc3qnQCpgPA565_565]_2-0" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHuss1985">Huss (1985)</a>, p.&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=NvEK7kc3qnQC&amp;pg=PA565">565</a>.</span>
</li>
